# Book 5: Fall 2015

## Chapter 1: A New Normal

### Part 1: The Dry Run

The Thursday before the Fall 2015 semester was set to begin, the entire Group of 8 stood at the Falling Branch Park & Ride, a tangible excitement buzzing between them. They had spent the summer settling into the Brooks basement, and now it was time to establish their new school-day routine.

"Okay," Zoe said, holding up her phone like a stopwatch. "The mission is to time the full morning commute. We need to know exactly how much time to give ourselves in the morning."

The first express bus to arrive was the RVT Roanoke Express. They all boarded for the quick 30-minute highway trip, which included a brief stop at New River Mall Christiansburg, before continuing on to the CPI Transit Center. From there, they spent the next hour walking around campus, verifying the buildings for their new classes, ensuring Jordan's accessibility accommodations were squared away, and visiting the University Card Center to officially switch to smaller meal plans now that they were commuting.

With their administrative tasks complete, they headed back to the transit center. The next bus back to Falling Branch was the Bluewater Transit Maroon Express. The 30-minute express ride was filled with a happy, productive chatter as they planned out their new domestic life.

"I'm so glad we switched to the smaller meal plan," Lauren said, looking at her updated student ID card. "It'll be nice to have dinner at home every night."

"Agreed," Aubrie said. "But it's good to have the breakfast and lunch option, just in case we're running late in the morning and don't have time to cook."

"I like it," Jordan said, a smile on his face. The simple act of planning their own meals, of making their own financial decisions, felt like a quiet victory.

The decision was made. They would all switch to a smaller meal plan, a practical choice that reflected their new status as independent homeowners. As the bus pulled into Falling Branch, Zoe clicked the stopwatch on her phone. They now had their baseline. They had a plan. They were ready for their sophomore year.

### Part 2: The Ocean and the Outer Banks

That evening, a feeling of peaceful, domestic contentment had settled over them in the Brooks' backyard.

Jordan, sitting in his chair with Aubrie at his side, watched the clouds drift by. Bolstered by the confidence from his new home and his successful planning day, he finally gave voice to a desire he had held silently for years.

"I've never seen the Atlantic Ocean," he said, his voice quiet.

Madison looked over from her lounge chair. "What? Really? Not even growing up in Hampton Roads?"

Jordan flinched slightly at the mention of his hometown. "My mother... she never took me. We were close, but... it was never a priority." He looked at the faces of his friends. "I'd really like to see it. The real ocean." He paused, his next words full of a quiet, firm boundary. "But... not there. Not Virginia Beach. I never want to go back."

An immediate, profound understanding passed through the group.

"Okay," Zoe said, her laptop already open on her lap. "So, new mission. Find an ocean that isn't Virginia Beach." She started typing. "The most direct route to the coast from here is U.S. 460 to I-64, which goes right through Hampton Roads..."

"No," Jordan said, a hint of panic in his voice. "I can't. I don't want to even drive through it."

"We won't," Zoe said immediately, already mapping a new course. "Okay, new plan. We can bypass it completely. We'll take I-81 south to I-77 south into North Carolina, then cut over on I-74 east to U.S. 52, then I-40, and finally U.S. 64 east all the way to the Outer Banks. It avoids the entire Hampton Roads area and the Chesapeake Expressway toll, and it only adds about a minute to the drive."

"The Outer Banks are perfect," Lauren said. "Kill Devil Hills is supposed to be really nice and family-friendly."

Jordan's eyes lit up. The idea was perfect. It wasn't just "not Virginia Beach"; it was a deliberate, safe passage to something new, something theirs.

"Can we?" he asked, looking at his friends, then instinctively toward the back door of the Brooks' house, where Daniel had just stepped out.

Daniel had overheard the last part of their conversation. He walked over, a warm smile on his face. "You want to go see the ocean, Jordan?" he asked. Jordan nodded.

Daniel's smile widened. "Then you're going to see the ocean. We'll rent a beach house in Kill Devil Hills for a couple of nights. We can all leave tomorrow morning, spend Friday and Saturday at the beach, and drive home on Sunday. That gives you one last weekend of freedom before classes start."

It was that simple. Jordan had voiced a desire, and his new family, without hesitation, re-routed their entire world to make it happen safely. They weren't just going to the beach. They were taking him to a new ocean, one that he could claim as his own.

### Part 3: The Detour

Sunday morning came too soon. After packing up their rental house in Kill Devil Hills, the group loaded into the two vehicles, a little sad to leave but filled with the happy, sun-drenched memories of the weekend.

As they got ready to pull out, Jordan spoke up from the passenger seat of the minivan, where Hannah was driving.

"Can we... can we try a different way home?" he asked, his voice hesitant. "The direct route. West on U.S. 158 to Barco, then take State Route 168 up through Chesapeake to I-64, and then take 460 home from there."

A heavy silence fell over the car. "Are you sure, Jordan?" Madison asked gently from the back seat.

"I want to try," he said, his voice gaining a bit of strength. "I need to see if I can just... drive through it. Focus on getting home."

Hannah looked at him, her eyes full of a deep, sisterly concern. She saw the resolve in his face. "Okay," she said softly. "We'll try. But the moment—the *second*—it's too much, we turn around. Deal?"

"Deal," he whispered.

Their spirits were high as they left Kill Devil Hills. They crossed the Wright Memorial Bridge, leaving the Outer Banks behind, and for the first thirty minutes of the drive, everything was fine. Jordan was focused, concentrating on the road ahead, on the promise of getting back to their new home.

As they neared the town of Barco, North Carolina, Hannah instinctively moved the minivan into the left-hand turn lane. Her instinct was correct.

A large, green overhead gantry sign came into view, spanning the highway. It showed an arrow pointing straight ahead for State Route 168 North to Norfolk. The left lane she was now in was marked for U.S. 158 West towards Elizabeth City.

The moment the sign registered, Jordan gasped, a sharp, ragged intake of breath. A low moan escaped his lips, and his hands flew up to cover his face as his body began to tremble. His attempt to face his demon had failed. The demon was too strong.

Hannah did not hesitate. She had already prepared for this outcome. She did not ask if he was okay. She just acted. When the traffic light turned green, she made the left turn, heading west onto U.S. 158, taking the southern route via Elizabeth City and U.S. 64 to avoid Hampton Roads entirely. The SUV behind them followed without question.

No one said a word. They didn't need to. Jordan had wanted to see if he could win a battle against his past, and he couldn't. And his family, his true family, was not going to subject him to a single moment more of that war.

## Chapter 2: The Sophomore Settle

### Part 1: The 5:45 AM Problem

The alarm on Zoe's mPhone was a shrill, invasive sound in the 5:00 AM darkness of the basement. Jordan Fuller groaned, burying his face in his pillow on the new, magnificent king-sized bed. He felt Aubrie stir beside him, and on his other side, Madison let out a noise that was a curse muffled by a comforter.

"Zoe," Madison mumbled, her voice thick with sleep. "You are an inhuman monster."

"It's 5:00 AM," Zoe replied from across the room, her voice already alert. The lights clicked on. "We walk to Falling Branch in twenty-five minutes. The Maroon Express departs at 5:45 AM."

Jordan sat up, rubbing his eyes. The entire Group of 8 was grumbling. This was Zoe's new decree for their sophomore year commute. Fearing "capacity issues" and the "parking panic" that choked the satellite lot, she had mandated they catch the very first express bus of the day. To Jordan, who was finally feeling safe, secure, and well-rested, a 5:00 AM wake-up felt like a betrayal.

Twenty-five minutes later, the eight of them were walking the dark, quiet blocks from Geneva Street to the Falling Branch Park & Ride. Jordan's hands were cold on the push-rings of his wheelchair as he propelled himself over the uneven pavement. This all felt like massive overkill.

Then, they rounded the corner into the Park & Ride.

Jordan stopped, his complaints dying in his throat. The massive commuter lot, under the bright orange glow of the highway lights, was already two-thirds full. And there, at the bus bay for the CPI-bound express routes, a small, tired line of about fifteen students and faculty had *already* formed.

"See?" Zoe said, her voice devoid of any "I told you so" smugness, stating it as a simple fact. "Logistical necessity."

Mark shook his head. "She's not human. It's not even 5:45."

When the large, highway-coach-style Bluewater Transit Maroon Express pulled in, the small line shuffled forward. The driver hopped out and saw Jordan, his practiced, tired eyes betraying no surprise. "Gotta use the lift, son," he called out. "Be just a minute."

Jordan hated this part. The local Blue Ridge Transit buses had simple, quick ramps. The long-haul coaches had a "slow, mechanical wheelchair lift" positioned in the center of the vehicle. The driver hit a switch, and the lift whirred to life, unfolding onto the pavement with a series of loud, attention-grabbing clunks. Jordan rolled onto the platform, was lifted up, and then had to maneuver into the securement space. By the time he was settled, the rest of his family had boarded, and the bus was already surprisingly full.

The bus pulled out, and Jordan let out a long breath. They had made it. They were safe, and they had space. He looked at Aubrie, who had taken the seat next to his securement spot, and she gave him a sleepy, reassuring smile.

The coach arrived at the CPI Transit Center at 6:15 AM.

The group disembarked—another slow, clunky process with the lift—and stood in the center of a dark, quiet campus.

"Okay," Madison said, pulling her hoodie tighter in the pre-dawn chill. "We're here. Now what?"

"We wait," Zoe said, checking her mPhone. "The library doesn't open for another forty-five minutes."

This was the trade-off. They had beaten the rush and guaranteed their spot, but in return, they had to wait for campus to wake up. They huddled in a covered bus bay, chatting quietly until the library unlocked its doors at 7:00 AM. They camped out at a table until 7:30 AM, when they migrated again to the Newman Dining Commons for breakfast.

Finally, at 8:45 AM, they headed to their first class: SOC 101.

Jordan wheeled beside Aubrie, their hands finding each other. This was their new normal. He thought about the looming threat of the IRS audit, the "paper invasion" his mother had initiated by lying on her tax return. It was that threat that had finalized his and Aubrie's decision to take every single class together. He would never have to face a professor or a classroom alone. She was his shield.

He looked at his seven friends, all walking with him, and felt a profound sense of security. The 5:00 AM alarm was a small price to pay for this.

### Part 2: The 45-Minute Wait

The slow, mechanical whine of the wheelchair lift was the only sound in the 6:15 AM stillness of the CPI Transit Center. Jordan maneuvered his chair onto the platform, was lowered to the pavement, and waited as the driver stowed the lift, a process that felt like an eternity in the pre-dawn chill.

As the Maroon Express pulled away, the handful of other passengers—mostly staff and contractors who kept the campus running—dispersed quickly into the darkness, their ID cards clicking as they unlocked the buildings they were there to open.

In seconds, the Group of 8 was left alone in the vast, empty transit center. The campus was dark and silent.

"Okay," Madison said, pulling her hoodie tighter and blowing into her hands. "We're here. Now what?"

Zoe, "inhuman monster" and architect of their morning, was already checking her mPhone. "We wait," she stated simply. "The library doesn't open for another forty-five minutes".

A collective groan went up from the group. "Forty-five minutes?" Lauren chafed. "We're just going to sit here in the cold?"

"This," Mark said, gesturing to their empty surroundings, "is the trade-off, I guess. No more rolling out of bed in 'The Old Lady' ten minutes before class".

This was the new hassle of commuting: the long, early bus ride from Christiansburg just to beat the crowds, only to arrive on a campus that was still asleep.

"It's not ideal, but it's secure," Zoe said, ever the pragmatist. "Let's find shelter."

She led them to a nearby bus bay that was shielded from the wind by thick brick walls on two sides. They huddled together on the cold metal benches, a small island of warmth and grumbling. Mark and Hannah shared a pair of earbuds, while Sarah and Lauren leaned against each other, trying to nap.

Jordan sat in the middle of it all, with Aubrie on the bench beside him, her head resting on his shoulder. He was tired, he was cold, and he wished he were back in the king-sized bed in the basement. But as he looked at his friends, all of them here with him, he felt a quiet, steady sense of rightness. This commute was a hassle, yes, but it was *their* hassle. It was the price of their freedom, and they were paying it together. He took Aubrie's hand, lacing his fingers through hers as they waited for the campus to wake up.

### Part 3: The Transit Center

Jordan sat huddled on the cold metal bench, his hand held firmly in Aubrie's. The pre-dawn chill of the New River Valley was sharp. Even though it was late August, these mountain mornings felt like deep autumn before the sun rose.

He glanced at his friends, all looking tired and cold, and then his gaze settled on Zoe. She was sitting slightly apart, her analytical focus absolute as her fingers flew across the screen of her tablet, sketching out the architecture for her Fin# project.

A profound wave of gratitude washed over Jordan, so strong it overshadowed the cold. Her family. They had taken him in. They had given him a home, a sanctuary, and were in the process of building him a future. Her logic, her family's generosity... Zoe was the architect of his freedom. She meant *so much* to him.

He needed to be connected to her, too. He reached out his free hand and tentatively touched her arm.

Zoe looked up from her tablet, her analytical gaze softening instantly. She saw his outstretched hand, processed the request, and understood. Her analytical mind processed the new input instantly. The request was logical. Jordan's emotional state was stable, and his motivation was clear: gratitude for her family's sanctuary and a need to reinforce the security of their pact. It was a positive data point. She glanced at the other observers—Madison, Lauren, and Sarah. They were smiling. Aubrie was stable. Her conclusion was immediate: The "Pact = true, Jealousy = false" axiom was confirmed and operating at peak efficiency, even in a public setting. She liked this data. It was an efficient system.

She didn't hesitate. She took his hand, lacing her fingers through his, her grip firm and secure. Then, amazingly, she allocated her left hand to him and resumed her work on her Fin# architecture with her right, as if holding his hand while architecting a new financial programming language was the most logical, normal thing in the world.

Jordan glanced over at the other women of the pact. Madison, Lauren, and Sarah were watching. They weren't whispering or glaring. They were smiling. He saw the pride in their eyes. There was no jealousy.

That single, terrifying thought—jealousy—felt like a ghost from another life. The intimate night they had all shared just before Fall Break, solidifying their new constitution, had made this simple, multi-partner act of comfort completely normal.

He was cold, yes, but he was also anchored, holding the hands of two of the women he loved, and he felt completely, utterly safe.

### Part 4: The Campus Wakes Up

The heavy glass doors of the library unlocked with an audible *click* precisely at 7:00 AM, signaling the official start of the campus day. The Group of 8, stiff and cold from their 45-minute wait, stood up and stretched.

"Okay, I don't know about you guys, but I need a bathroom," Mark announced to the group.

Jordan nodded. This was the new logistical hurdle of his commuter life, but one his family had already solved. "I've got you, man," Mark said, his voice easy and reassuring. He accompanied Jordan to the nearest men's restroom, assisting him in the handicap stall with the same quiet, brotherly trust they had established at the mall.

When they all regrouped a few minutes later, the consensus was unanimous: they were hungry, and the cafeteria opened in twenty minutes. "A hot breakfast is the logical next step," Zoe affirmed.

"I'll push," Lauren said, stepping behind Jordan's chair. She and Hannah, as the strongest women in the group, had naturally fallen into the role of managing his wheelchair on the notoriously hilly CPI campus.

The group moved out, a familiar, tight-knit unit. As they started up the first major incline, Jordan felt a profound sense of peace. He was back. He was free. He reached out his hands. To his left, Sarah took his hand, her grip warm and steady. To his right, Madison did the same.

Aubrie, walking just ahead with Zoe, glanced back. She saw Jordan holding hands with two other women of their pact, and her face broke into a brilliant, loving smile. Madison, catching Aubrie's eye, beamed back. There was no jealousy, only the deep, shared joy of seeing the man they loved so happy and secure.

Their path took them right past their old home, Bennett Hall. Jordan stopped for a second. The "Old Lady on the Hill" was gone. In her place was a tall construction fence, surrounding a deep pit of dirt and rubble. The dorm was officially being torn down, set to be replaced by a modern residence hall. A strange pang of nostalgia hit him. They were among the last students to ever sleep in those rooms, the last to use that flawed connecting bathroom. That building, and the clerical error that put him there, was where his entire life had truly begun.

Lauren pushed him the last few yards to the Newman Dining Commons. They arrived with a few minutes to spare, joining a small line of other early-bird students, ready for a warm breakfast before their first class.

### Part 5: A Flaw in the System

The eight friends huddled around a table in the Newman Dining Commons, the warmth of the cafeteria and their hot breakfast a welcome relief. It was 7:35 AM. They had been on campus for an hour and twenty minutes, having spent the first forty-five minutes waiting in a cold, dark bus shelter for the library to open.

"I'm never going to get used to that 5:00 AM alarm," Mark grumbled, taking a large bite of scrambled eggs.

"It's just inhuman," Madison agreed, nursing a large coffee. "I get *why*, Zoe, but... ouch. We still have an hour and a half until our first class."

"It was the only logical solution to guarantee capacity on the express coach," Zoe stated, her tone matter-of-fact as she ate her oatmeal. "The 6:45 AM and 7:15 AM buses would have been at standing load."

"It's fine for today," Hannah said, setting her fork down. Her "mom friend" instincts were overriding her exhaustion. "But I'm worried about the long term. It's late August and it was *freezing* out there this morning. What are we going to do when it's November and pouring rain? Or January and snowing?".

The table went quiet. She was right.

Hannah looked directly at Jordan, her expression full of a deep, sisterly concern. "We can't have him sitting outside a locked library for forty-five minutes in the freezing cold, Zoe. Not when he's still medically vulnerable".

This was the variable Zoe's pure logic had missed. The group knew Jordan was in the middle of his long, multi-year "catch-up" vaccination schedule. His mother's anti-vax stance meant he was still vulnerable to illnesses that the rest of them took for granted. A simple flu, which he had already battled once, could be a serious medical event for him.

Zoe stopped eating. Hannah was correct. Her current solution minimized commute-time congestion but maximized environmental exposure. It was an unacceptable risk to the pact's most important member.

"You're right," Zoe said, her voice quiet as her mind immediately began re-calculating. "The 5:45 AM departure is not a sustainable all-weather solution. It fails to balance efficiency with Jordan's medical needs".

Jordan looked at Hannah gratefully. He had been dreading the coming winter, but now the problem was out in the open. They had to find a new plan—one that still beat the rush but didn't leave them stranded in the cold.

### Part 6: A New System Variable

As the group finished their breakfast, the logistical problem hung in the air: Zoe’s 5:45 AM plan was efficient for beating the "standing load", but it was a failure in terms of Jordan's health and safety.

"Okay," Aubrie said, taking Jordan's hand. "So the 5:45 AM express is out. We can't have him waiting in the snow for 45 minutes. It's not an option."

"Agreed," Mark chimed in. "So what's the next bus?"

Zoe, who had been silently processing this new "medical vulnerability" variable, pulled up the schedules on her mPhone. Her original calculation was flawed because it had prioritized *capacity* over *environment*. She now had to find the optimal balance.

"The library opens at 7:00 AM," Zoe stated, analyzing the data. "We must arrive *after* that time, but *before* the 6:45 AM peak begins. This is a logical contradiction. The two events do not overlap."

"So, what does that mean?" Lauren asked.

"It means," Zoe continued, "we must accept a minimal, controlled wait. The 5:45 AM express arrives at 6:15 AM, leaving a 45-minute environmental exposure time. This is inefficient and dangerous."

She scrolled to the next departure. "The 6:00 AM Route 1 local departs 15 minutes later and arrives on campus at 6:45 AM. This reduces the outdoor wait time to only 15 minutes—a 66.7% reduction in exposure."

Jordan looked at the group. "I can do 15 minutes," he said, his voice quiet but firm. "I can't do 45."

"But won't that bus be crowded?" Madison asked. "It's closer to the rush."

"No," Zoe said, shaking her head. "The data shows the peak *starts* at 6:45 AM. The 6:00 AM local bus departs *before* the 'parking panic' wave truly begins. It is the most logical compromise."

A wave of relief passed through the group. They had a new plan.

"It's a longer ride," Mark noted. "45 minutes on a local bus instead of 30 on the express."

"It's a worthwhile trade-off," Aubrie said immediately, looking at Jordan. "His health is more important than 15 minutes."

The decision was unanimous. Their new wake-up call would be 5:15 AM.

### Part 7: The Sociology Fortress

Breakfast at the Newman Dining Commons finished just before 8:00 AM. The Group of 8 still had an hour to wait before their first class of the day. This was the new, inefficient reality of their commute, a trade-off for the safety of the Brooks' basement and the necessity of beating the morning rush.

The class they shared, SOC 101 (Introductory Sociology), was a classic sophomore requirement, held in one of the largest lecture halls on campus.

As they walked across campus, Jordan could feel the familiar, low-grade anxiety thrumming under his skin. A large, crowded lecture hall was a sensory minefield of noise, motion, and flickering fluorescent lights—a known trigger for both him and Aubrie.

They arrived at the hall at 8:45 AM, and it was already a swirling chaos of students filing in, shouting greetings, and slamming down laptops. Jordan instinctively reached for Aubrie's hand, who in turn gripped Zoe's.

This was the group's first test of the semester. They moved as a single, cohesive unit, their mission clear: secure a defensible position. They headed for the designated accessible seating area at the back of the tiered auditorium.

"Okay, here," Mark said, directing traffic. He and Lauren, the group's physical "flanks," positioned themselves on the outer chairs of the row, creating a small, protective wall. Zoe and Sarah filed in next.

This allowed Jordan to wheel into the open wheelchair space, with Aubrie sitting on one side of him and Madison on the other, cocooning him from the worst of the noise. Hannah sat at the end, completing their "fortress." They had successfully claimed an entire row, a bubble of safety in the overwhelming environment.

At 9:00 AM on the dot, the professor began the lecture. The class was a standard 50-minute session. Jordan, anchored by Aubrie's hand on one side and Madison's on the other, was able to focus. He was safe. He was surrounded by his family, and no one could get to him.

## Chapter 3: The Paper Invasion

### Part 1: The Letter

It happened on a quiet Tuesday evening in October. The Group of 8 had settled into a comfortable routine, living in their fortress in the Brooks' family basement. Some were upstairs in the kitchen helping Laura with dinner prep, while others were in the basement, doing homework and relaxing.

Upstairs, Daniel was sorting through the day's mail when he stopped, a heavy feeling settling in his stomach. He held up a letter for Laura to see. It was a crisp, official-looking envelope from the Department of the Treasury, Internal Revenue Service. And it was addressed to Aubrey Jordan Fuller.

Laura’s face paled. They both knew that name was a landmine.

They didn't just hand it over. They went downstairs and found Jordan in the main basement area, chatting with Aubrie Kelly and Madison. The four of them—Laura, Daniel, Aubrie, and Madison—formed a quiet, protective circle around him.

"Jordan," Daniel began gently, his voice calm and steady. "A piece of official mail came for you today." He paused, giving Jordan a moment to prepare. "It's from the IRS, and... it has your legal name on it."

Jordan's breath hitched. He looked at the envelope Daniel was holding out, and the sight of the name—his old name, the name his mother used, the name he was trying to legally kill—was like a physical blow. The world tilted. His carefully constructed peace shattered.

The meltdown was severe. His body trembled, and a low, keening sound of pure distress escaped his lips. Aubrie and Madison were there instantly, their hands on his shoulders, grounding him. Daniel and Laura stood as silent, steady sentinels, ensuring he was safe. They didn't try to stop it; they simply weathered the storm with him.

### Part 2: The Battle Plan

After what felt like an eternity, the worst of the storm passed. The trembling subsided into shuddering breaths. The keening faded into exhausted silence. He looked at the letter, which now lay on the table beside him, and the fear in his eyes was replaced by a cold, hard anger. He opened it.

He looked up at the four faces surrounding him, his protectors, his family. "What happened?" he finally asked, his voice raw but firm. "What is a CP87A? Why did they send me this? And what do we need to do to fight back?"

Laura pulled a chair over, sitting beside Jordan so she could look him directly in the eye. "Okay," she began, her voice the epitome of calm competence. "A CP87A is not a bill or a penalty. It's a notice. It just means that the IRS computers have found a problem: two different people filed tax returns claiming the same person as a dependent."

Jordan’s jaw tightened. "My mother." It wasn't a question.

"Yes," Daniel said, his voice flat and hard. "She must have filed her taxes and claimed you, even though she knew you were claiming yourself. She either lied, or she's so deep in her own world she thinks she's still entitled to."

"So... what happens now?" Madison asked, her hand finding Jordan's and squeezing it tightly.

"Now, we prove them wrong," Laura said, her tone shifting from explanation to strategy. "The notice gives us two options. Option one is to amend your tax return and agree that your mother can claim you. We are not doing that." Her voice was firm, leaving no room for doubt.

"Option two," she continued, "is to do nothing. If neither person backs down, the IRS will audit both of you to determine who is actually eligible to claim the dependent. And based on the law—your age, the fact that you live independently and provide more than half of your own support—you are the only one who can legally claim the exemption. She has no case."

Jordan looked from Laura to Daniel, then to Aubrie and Madison. His fear, which had been a raging fire, was now being banked by the calm, steady confidence of his family.

"So we do nothing?" Jordan asked, his voice still shaky but gaining strength. "We just... wait?"

"We don't just wait," Daniel said. "We prepare. We gather all your documents—your lease from the dorm, your bank statements, your school registration. We build a file. Then we sit and wait." He met Jordan's eyes. "And Jordan, you need to hear this. This is going to be a long, slow, drawn-out process. The IRS moves at its own pace. It might be months, maybe even close to a year, before you get the next letter. So long, in fact, that you'll probably forget all about it. We'll have everything ready, but you cannot let this hang over your head. You have to live your life."

He squeezed Jordan's shoulder. "She wanted a fight. Now she's got one. And this time, she's picking a fight with the United States government."

## Chapter 4: Fall Break 2015

### Part 1: The Gauntlet

It was Fall Break of their sophomore year, and the Group of 8 was on a mission to see the autumn leaves. Their plan was to drive north to Afton Mountain and spend the day on the scenic Blue Ridge Parkway.

The trip up I-81 was beautiful, the Shenandoah Valley painted in vibrant strokes of red, orange, and gold. But for Jordan, sitting in the passenger seat of the SUV with Mark driving, the beauty was tainted by a creeping dread. He was already on edge. They had just endured the "30-mile gauntlet"—the long, unavoidable concurrency of I-81 North and I-64 East between Lexington and Staunton. He had held up, but it was a near-constant, low-grade hum of anxiety.

As they approached Staunton, Mark put on his turn signal. "Okay, J," he said softly, "this is it. Exit 221. We're getting on I-64 East, just for a few miles to get to the Parkway entrance. You ready?"

Jordan gave a shaky nod, gripping the armrest. "I'm ready." He was trying to be brave.

The moment the SUV curved onto the long exit ramp for I-64 East toward Richmond, his fragile composure shattered. It was too much. The sign, the direction, the feeling of being pulled back toward the place of his nightmares—it all crashed down on him at once.

He went from being on edge straight into a severe autistic meltdown. A raw, ragged sob tore from his throat, and he began to cry uncontrollably, his body shaking with the force of it. His mind was no longer in the car; it was back in Hampton Roads, flooded with a torrent of painful memories of everything his mother had done to him.

Sarah, sitting in the back seat directly behind him, reacted instantly. She unbuckled, leaned forward, and wrapped her arms around him, holding him as tightly as she could. "I've got you, Jordan," she whispered, her voice a steady anchor in his storm. "I've got you." She had to physically restrain him as best she could in the vehicle as it merged onto the interstate, moving at the 70-mile-per-hour speed limit.

Mark, seeing what was happening, didn't hesitate. His eyes darted to the next highway sign. Exit 91: Fishersville/Stuarts Draft. He stomped on the accelerator, getting ahead of a semi-truck, and then cut across the lane to the exit ramp.

At the top of the ramp, the familiar golden arches of a fast-food restaurant promised a safe harbor. Mark pulled the SUV into the parking lot of the "Rocket Burger" and threw it into park.

The car was silent except for Jordan's ragged sobs. The group sat there for a long time, Sarah still holding him, until the worst of the meltdown had passed.

In the quiet aftermath, a silent, unanimous decision was made. They would find another way to the Parkway. They would never, ever subject Jordan to the I-64 trigger again. Some demons were too powerful to fight. The only winning move was not to play.

They sat in the "Rocket Burger" parking lot for a long time, the only sound Jordan's ragged sobs as they slowly subsided. Sarah never let go of him.

After the worst of the meltdown had passed, Zoe, from the back of the SUV, quietly cleared her throat. She had her phone out, a map displayed on the screen.

"Okay," she said softly, so as not to startle Jordan. "I have a new route. We can take this road, State Route 285, through Fishersville until it hits U.S. 250. Then we take 250 west through Waynesboro, and it will take us directly to the Rockfish Gap entrance of the Blue Ridge Parkway. We never have to get back on I-64."

Mark looked at Jordan, whose face was still buried in Sarah's shoulder. "What do you think, J? Do you want to try that, or do you just want to go home?"

Jordan took a shaky breath and gave a small, almost imperceptible nod. He still wanted to see the leaves.

"Okay," Mark said, his voice full of a gentle resolve. "New plan it is."

### Part 2: The Overlook

Inside the "Rocket Burger," the group found a quiet booth in the back. They ate their food in relative silence, the normal, easy chatter replaced by a protective quiet. Sarah sat beside Jordan, her arm a constant, reassuring presence around his shoulders, hugging him for as long as he needed before they got back on the road.

When it was time to leave, they rearranged the seating. Jordan moved to the minivan to sit next to Aubrie, while Sarah now rode with Mark. The minivan followed the SUV as they took the new, safe route: State Route 285 to U.S. 250, through the small towns of Fishersville and Waynesboro. Zoe held Jordan tightly the entire way as they climbed the mountain toward the Blue Ridge Parkway.

Their first stop was the Afton Overlook. Mark pulled the SUV into a parking spot, and the view was breathtaking—a panoramic vista of the Shenandoah Valley, ablaze with the colors of autumn.

They got out of the vehicles, but Jordan was still tense, the residual adrenaline from the meltdown leaving him shaky. As he sat in his wheelchair, taking in the view, Zoe and Sarah stood on either side of him, their hands on his shoulders, holding him tightly.

A moment later, the rest of the group walked over from the other vehicle, their faces etched with concern. Mark quietly explained to them what had happened, how the very short, one-exit ride on I-64 had triggered such a severe meltdown.

As Mark finished his explanation, Aubrie and Madison moved in, taking over the comforting hugs from Zoe and Sarah. They knelt on either side of his chair, their presence a warm, protective shield.

"We don't have to stay, J," Aubrie whispered. "We can go home right now if you want."

Jordan took a deep, shaky breath, the crisp mountain air filling his lungs. He looked at the vast, beautiful valley below, then at the faces of his seven friends, his family, all surrounding him.

"No," he said, his voice quiet but firm. "No. I want to see the leaves." He looked at Aubrie. "I'll ride with you."

The plan was adjusted once more. They would continue south on the Blue Ridge Parkway as intended. Then, to get home, they would take U.S. Route 60 west through Buena Vista to Lexington. This route would allow them to get back on I-81 south of the 30-mile gauntlet, ensuring Jordan never had to be on I-64 again that day.

He was still fragile, still recovering, but he was not broken. Surrounded by his wall of love and support, he was ready to reclaim the rest of his day.

### Part 3: The Basement Sanctuary

The drive back from Afton Mountain was long and profoundly quiet, a stark contrast to the cheerful anticipation of the morning. The winding path down U.S. 60 from the Blue Ridge Parkway had been beautiful, but the residual tension from Jordan's meltdown was a heavy blanket in both vehicles. When the convoy finally pulled onto Geneva Street, a collective, unspoken sigh of relief filled the air. They were back at the Brooks’ house, the first place Jordan had truly thought of as a home, a sanctuary.

The other members of the group, understanding Jordan was emotionally and physically depleted, gave him space. He was still fragile, the tremors from the I-64 trigger still humming beneath his skin. He headed straight for the basement, his fortress, needing to decompress. He transferred from his chair to one of the four sturdy queen-sized beds that had replaced their old air mattresses. He lay on his side, curled up, but the anxiety wouldn't abate.

He looked up as Aubrie entered the room, her face etched with a deep, loving concern.

"Hey," she whispered, coming to sit on the edge of the bed. "How are you doing? For real."

He just shook his head, the simple question too big to answer. "Will you..." he asked, his voice quiet and raw, "will you lie down with me? Just for a little while. I just... I need you here."

"Of course," Aubrie said, not hesitating for a second. She slipped off her shoes and lay down on the bed beside him, pulling the comforter over them both. She didn't crowd him, just lay at his side, her presence a warm, steady anchor in the storm that was still swirling in his mind.

A moment later, Madison, his other original roommate, came over. "How's our guy doing?" she asked softly. She sat on the edge of the bed by Jordan's other side, a quiet, protective guardian.

"Trying not to think about it," Jordan mumbled, his face half-buried in the pillow as he fought the memory of the highway sign.

Aubrie, knowing exactly what he needed, shifted the topic to the safest, most logical place she knew. "Did you finish the reading for Dr. Alistair's class?" she asked, her voice light. "The new chapter on red giants? I was still a little confused about the helium flash..."

Jordan turned his head, his mind gratefully latching onto the safe, orderly topic. "It's the electron-degenerate matter," he said, his voice a little stronger. "The core doesn't expand when it heats up, so the fusion just... runs away. It’s not like a normal star".

"See, I'm glad you said that," Madison chimed in, her interest genuine. "I was trying to read that chapter for fun just to keep up with you two, and I thought 'helium flash' sounded like something from a superhero movie."

Jordan and Aubrie both let out small, watery laughs. "It's not just you," Aubrie smiled. "It's really complex."

Madison, seeing them both finally begin to relax, decided to join them fully. "Scooch over, J," she said, lying down on his other side.

The three of them lay there, talking quietly about stellar evolution, neutron stars, and the comforting, predictable laws of the cosmos. The conversation, so logical and vast, was the perfect antidote to the sharp, specific terror of the interstate. Jordan was in the middle, cocooned between the two women who had been with him since the beginning.

He closed his eyes, the heavy weight in his chest finally beginning to lift. The last time he had felt this profoundly safe, this completely and utterly grounded, was the day he met them. It was the same overwhelming feeling of security he'd had when Aubrie first wrapped her arms around him during his move-in day meltdown.

He trusted everyone in this house—Daniel, Laura, Zoe, Lauren, Hannah, Sarah, and Mark—with his entire life. But these two... they were different. They were his origin story. In the quiet of his own mind, a new, definitive truth settled, clear and bright as a constellation.

*They are my safe people,* he thought, a profound sense of peace washing over him. *My autistic safe people.*

He didn't need to say it aloud. Lying there between them, the tremors finally stopped, and the lingering fear of I-64 dissolved, replaced by the quiet, steady, and unconditional gravity of the two women who held his new universe together.

### Part 4: The Sickness

The quiet sanctuary in the Brooks’ basement, where Jordan, Aubrie, and Madison had been lost in a comforting discussion of astrophysics, was interrupted by a cheerful call from the top of the internal stairs.

"Dinner's ready!" Laura Brooks’s voice echoed down. "Come and get it!"

Aubrie and Madison stretched, smiling as they sat up. "Ready to face the world, J?" Madison asked gently.

Jordan nodded, but as he pushed himself up to sit on the edge of the queen bed, a wave of dizziness washed over him. The room tilted, and he had to brace himself with his arms. He’d felt "off" since the drive, but he had assumed it was just the lingering exhaustion from the meltdown on the highway. This felt different. This was a physical, disorienting wooziness.

"Whoa," he said, his voice quiet. "Just... give me a second."

Aubrie, who was already moving to bring his wheelchair closer, stopped instantly. "Jordan? Are you okay?"

He looked pale, and not just the "post-meltdown" pale she was used to. There was an unhealthy, faint flush high on his cheeks. Madison, who had been lying beside him, pressed the back of her hand to his forehead.

"J, you're burning up," she said, her playful tone gone, replaced by immediate, sharp concern.

The quiet shift in the basement quickly drew the others. Hannah, the group's quintessential "mom friend", came over from the other side of the room. "What's going on?"

"He feels really warm," Aubrie said, her own hand now on his neck.

Hannah’s expression turned serious. "Zoe, can you grab the digital thermometer from the bathroom cabinet?"

A moment later, Zoe returned. Hannah unwrapped the thermometer, placed it under Jordan's tongue, and they waited in silence. The device finally let out a small *beep*. Hannah pulled it out and read the display. Her eyes widened slightly.

"100.1," she announced to the room.

A different kind of anxiety settled over the group. This wasn't the aftermath of a psychological trigger; this was a new, physical threat.

"Oh, Jordan," Laura Brooks said, having come downstairs to see what the delay was. She saw the thermometer in Hannah's hand and the miserable look on his face. Her mind went straight to the immunization schedule she and Daniel were so carefully helping him manage. This was, after all, the same young man who had nearly been hospitalized by the flu just last year because his mother had refused to vaccinate him.

"This isn't the meltdown, is it, honey?" she asked gently.

Jordan shook his head, looking exhausted. "I just feel... dizzy. And hot."

Laura nodded, her face full of a new, grim understanding. "Okay," she said, her voice full of calm authority. "This is just one last ghost from your mother's past we have to get through. You're on the catch-up schedule, but you're not fully protected yet. It looks like you caught one more bug before we could finish."

The dinner plan was instantly revised.

"You are not going anywhere," Aubrie said firmly. "Madison, can you help me get him comfortable?"

The two of them, his "safest people", helped him settle back down, propping him up against the pillows.

"I'll bring you some soup and toast from upstairs," Laura said, already heading for the stairs.

Jordan lay back against the pillows, exhausted from the day and now feeling the heavy, aching onset of the fever. The terror of I-64 felt a million miles away, replaced by the simple, miserable reality of being sick. But as he looked at Aubrie and Madison sitting on either side of his bed, he knew he was safe. The meltdown had passed, and now, his family would simply weather this new storm with him, just as they always did.

### Part 5: The Fever and the Fortress

Laura returned to the basement a few minutes later, Daniel right behind her. They carried a tray with a steaming bowl of chicken noodle soup, a sleeve of saltine crackers, and a glass of dry ginger ale.

"Alright, honey," Laura said softly. "Let's try and get some of this into you."

Jordan pushed himself up against the pillows, but the movement made the room swim. He felt like he was burning up from the inside out. Aubrie and Madison, his two safest people in the world, wouldn't leave his side. They sat flanking him on the queen bed, a quiet, protective perimeter. Aubrie gently took the tray from Laura and placed it on his lap.

He took a few sips of the ginger ale, the cool carbonation a small relief. "I can't be sick," he whispered, his voice raspy. "We have class on Wednesday."

"Don't you worry about Wednesday," Daniel said firmly, sitting at the foot of the bed. "You just worry about resting. You know what this is, Jordan. It's just your body fighting off one last thing your mother left you vulnerable to. You're getting your catch-up shots, but you're not fully armored yet. This is just... the last battle."

The word "battle" seemed to flip a switch in his feverish mind. His anxiety, which had been a low hum of physical misery, suddenly sharpened, latching onto the other, larger threat looming over him.

"The letter," he said, his eyes finding Daniel's. "The CP87A. What if... what if they call me to an audit and I'm sick? What if she uses this? What if she tells them I can't even handle a cold?"

"Hey, stop right there," Daniel said, his voice cutting through the rising panic. "We talked about this. That fight isn't for today. We have all our paperwork ready to roll. We have your lease, your bank statements, your class schedule... we have everything. All we do now is wait for the IRS to do their audit, and when they do, we'll bury her in facts. She has no case. You've got this."

Jordan nodded, but the reassurance, as logical as it was, couldn't penetrate the fog of the fever. His nerves felt raw, exposed.

Seeing the fear still in his eyes, Aubrie and Madison moved in closer. They didn't say anything. They just held him. Aubrie wrapped her arms around his back from one side, and Madison mirrored her from the other, their combined embrace a firm, steady pressure.

He sank into their hold, closing his eyes. The logical words from Daniel were helpful, but this... this was what he needed. He felt their hugs pressing the frayed, anxious nerves back into his mind, calming the static. He was safe. He was burning up, and he was worried, but he was home, and his two most trusted people in the world were literally holding him together.

### Part 6: The Fortress of Comfort

Saturday evening settled over the Brooks' house, but the usual happy, chaotic energy was muted. After a small, forced dinner of soup, crackers, and dry ginger ale, Jordan was clearly fading. The low-grade fever, combined with the profound emotional exhaustion from the Afton Mountain incident and the lingering anxiety about the IRS notice, had left him feeling utterly depleted. His muscles ached, and his limbs felt heavy and uncooperative.

"I think... I think I just need to lie down for good," he mumbled, his voice raspy. "I'm just... done."

Getting him ready for bed required "all hands on deck". His fever made him weak and shaky, and he didn't have the strength to manage the dressing techniques he had been so proud of learning. There was no hesitation from the group. Mark and Hannah provided the steady strength to help him stand and pivot, while Aubrie and Madison, his "safest people," handled the gentle, close work of helping him out of his day clothes and into soft pajamas. Zoe and Lauren prepped his bed with fresh blankets, and Sarah got his medications and a fresh glass of water. He felt miserable, but the group’s seamless, judgment-free teamwork kept any feelings of humiliation at bay.

With Jordan finally settled in one of the queen-sized portable beds, the rest of the group looked for a way to wind down.

"Well, tomorrow is a wash," Mark said, stretching. "We're not taking him anywhere with a fever. We need a serious, all-day distraction."

He walked over to the large, well-stocked media shelf in the basement living area, scanning the titles. Suddenly, he stopped. "No. Way," he breathed, pulling a large, deluxe Blu-ray box set from the shelf. He held it up for the room to see.

"Is that...?" Madison asked, her eyes going wide.

Mark grinned, tapping the cover. "The Silver Gauntlet of Aethel: 25th Anniversary Definitive Aethelian Tome Edition".

"Oh, I *love* that movie!" Hannah exclaimed. "It is of my all-time favorites. That 'A Sister's Love' theme song is pure 80s cheesy fantasy bliss!".

"Mine too!" Sarah agreed. "It’s just so perfectly epic".

"This isn't just the theatrical cut, ladies," Mark said with reverence. "This is the 2012 Blu-ray. This is the legendary *Leviathan Cut*."

A respectful silence fell over the group. "The 12-hour, 21-minute one?" Zoe asked, her analytical interest clearly piqued.

"The very one," Mark confirmed. "We are *so* watching this tomorrow. It's the perfect sick-day movie. This will be the first time any of us have seen it."

The decision was unanimous. Tomorrow would be dedicated to Kaelan, Kaela, and the epic synthesizer score of *The Silver Gauntlet of Aethel*.

With a plan set for the next day, the group began to prepare for the night. There was no question about the sleeping arrangements. Jordan was sick, feverish, and anxious.

"Madi, you take that side," Aubrie said quietly, pointing to the left side of Jordan's bed. Madison nodded, grabbing her pillow.

Aubrie slid in on Jordan's right. He was already half-asleep, burning up between them. He mumbled, still fighting the residual anxiety from the day. "The letter... she's not..."

"Shh," Aubrie whispered, putting a cool hand on his forehead. "We've got you. Daniel's right. We're ready for her. Just rest."

Madison wrapped an arm around his other side. He was cocooned, physically held by his two autistic safe people. He finally let go, sinking into the fever and the profound, absolute safety of their embrace.

### Part 7: Sunday – The Long Wait

Jordan woke on Sunday morning to a world of muffled aches and a heavy, feverish warmth. He was still cocooned between his two safest people. Aubrie was on his right, her breathing soft and steady, and Madison was on his left, her arm draped protectively over his chest. He felt miserable, but the bone-deep terror from the day before was gone, held at bay by their combined presence.

"Morning, sicky," Madison murmured, her voice thick with sleep as she felt him stir. "How's the fever?"

"Still here," Jordan rasped, his throat sore. "I feel like I'm burning up".

"Okay, first things first," Aubrie said, sitting up and pushing her dark hair from her face. "Operation: Get Cleaned Up. We're not going anywhere today, so we're all staying in pajamas, but nobody's going to feel better until they've had a shower".

The process was a slow, coordinated effort. While the rest of the group took turns in the basement's accessible shower, Aubrie and Madison focused on Jordan. His fever left him weak and dizzy, so they patiently helped him, their actions a seamless blend of intimacy and practical care, reinforcing the trust of their pact.

While they were busy, Mark took charge of getting the main living area situated for their marathon movie day. He fluffed pillows on the large sectional sofa, set up extra blankets on the portable beds, and meticulously hooked up the Blu-ray player. He slotted the first disc of *The Silver Gauntlet of Aethel: 25th Anniversary "Definitive Aethelian Tome" Edition*, a look of reverent excitement on his face.

Just as the group, now all in fresh, comfortable pajamas, was helping Jordan get settled into a nest of blankets on the sofa, Daniel and Laura came down the internal stairs. They were carrying trays laden with a full, hot breakfast—scrambled eggs, toast, bacon, and fruit for the group, and a simple, comforting bowl of oatmeal and more dry ginger ale for Jordan.

"Heard we have a long day of 80s cheese ahead of us," Daniel said with a warm smile. "You're going to need provisions."

The group ate together, the atmosphere cozy and safe. The fear of I-64 and the anxiety of the IRS letter were still present, but they were outside the walls of this basement sanctuary.

"Alright," Mark said, taking the empty plates. "The moment of truth. Twelve hours and twenty-one minutes of pure, unadulterated cheesy fantasy bliss. Everybody ready?"

Jordan, tucked securely between Aubrie and Madison on the sofa, pulled a blanket up to his chin and nodded. He was sick, he was exhausted, but he was home.

Mark hit "play." The lights dimmed, and the room filled with the soaring, epic synthesizer score of "A Sister's Love (Main Theme)," officially beginning their long day's journey into the Gauntlet.

### Part 8: The Leviathan Unveiled

The epic, synthesizer-driven anthem of "A Sister's Love (Main Theme)" finally faded, and the opening chapter title appeared on the screen: Book I: The Sundering of Sunstead. A quiet, reverent hush settled over the basement. This was the first time any of them had seen the legendary Leviathan Cut, and the atmosphere was one of shared discovery.

Jordan, still feverish and miserable, was anchored firmly between Aubrie and Madison on the sofa. The movie's vast, logical world was the perfect medicine, his mind focusing on the intricate new details rather than his own anxiety.

For the veteran fans of the theatrical release—Hannah, Sarah, and Madison—the experience was a revelation.

"Wait, *this* is how it starts?" Hannah whispered, her eyes wide with awe as they watched the opening scenes in the gleaming Royal Court of Old Aethel. "I've never seen any of this!"

"They just... cut out Malakor's entire origin story?" Madison whispered back, scandalized. "And the prophecy? That explains so much!"

They watched, completely engrossed, as the film delved deep into the twins' childhood. They saw Kaelan and Kaela as infants, arriving in Sunstead, and their cramped, loving upbringing in the shepherd's hut. When the film showed the 18-year-old twins in the hut, forced to share a single bed, a new, profound understanding settled over the Room 214 trio. Jordan, Aubrie, and Madison exchanged a quiet, knowing look. They understood that feeling of a "cage of comfort," of a shared, cramped space forging an unbreakable, complex bond.

The hours of the morning melted away. They watched Kaelan's guilt-ridden vow and his first trials in the Whispering Woods of Whispers. The light, whispered movie talk was mostly from the veteran fans.

"See, that's new," Sarah would murmur, pointing at the screen. "In the theatrical cut, he just finds the old hermit. This whole sequence with the voices... it makes so much more sense now."

Jordan remained quiet, his fever a dull, hot thrum in the background. He was grateful for the distraction and even more grateful for the steady, warm presence of Aubrie on his left and Madison on his right. He was safe.

Finally, the screen faded to black, and a title card appeared: *End of Book I*. Mark paused the movie, and the group stirred, blinking in the low light of the basement as if waking from a dream.

"Wow," Hannah said, stretching. "That was... a lot. And that's just the first part?"

Right on cue, Daniel Brooks's voice called down the stairs. "How's the Gauntlet going, team? It's lunchtime. I can bring some food down for everyone if you're ready to pause for a bit."

### Part 9: The Mid-Gauntlet Intermission

"Yes, please, Mr. Brooks!" Madison called up the stairs, her voice cheerful. "We're starving!"

Mark hit the pause button on the Blu-ray remote, freezing the screen on the *End of Book I* title card. The marathon was officially on intermission. As the group stirred, stretching after hours of being engrossed, the first priority was a collective trip to the basement's shared bathroom.

Jordan, however, remained on the sofa, the effort to even sit up sending a wave of dizziness through him. The fever was making his limbs feel like lead. He looked over at Hannah, who immediately understood.

"We've got you, J," she said, nodding to Lauren. As the two strongest women in the group, they instinctively took the lead in helping him when he was physically at his lowest.

Mark watched as his girlfriend, Hannah, and her suitemate, Lauren, moved to Jordan's side. They helped him transfer from the sofa to his wheelchair and then wheeled him toward the bathroom, their movements a seamless, practiced dance of support as they prepared to assist him with his toileting needs. Mark felt a familiar surge of pride. He saw Hannah step up to care for their friend in a deeply personal way, and his only thought was how lucky he was to be with someone so boundlessly compassionate.

When Hannah emerged a few minutes later, she caught his eye and gave him a warm, private smile. She felt a corresponding wave of gratitude for his unwavering trust, a stark and welcome contrast to the toxic, insecure jealousy that had defined Lauren's relationship with Tom.

Soon, Daniel and Laura came down with trays of food. For the seven healthy movie-watchers, it was a perfect, easy lunch of sandwiches, chips, and various sodas. For Jordan, Laura brought a separate tray: another steaming bowl of chicken noodle soup, a fresh sleeve of saltine crackers, and a cold glass of dry ginger ale.

The group, still in their pajamas, settled back into their comfortable nest of blankets. Jordan was propped up on the sofa again, with Aubrie and Madison on either side, as he slowly ate his soup.

"I still can't believe they cut Malakor's entire origin story from the theatrical release," Madison said, taking a large bite of her sandwich. "It makes his villain song so much more impactful!"

"And the way they introduced the twins' parents fleeing the capital," Hannah added. "It completely changes Kaelan's journey. He's not just some kid; he's royalty in hiding".

Jordan listened, his fever a dull thrum in the background, but his mind was calm. He was safe, he was fed, and his family was dissecting the "absolute 1980s cheesy fantasy bliss" of a 12-hour movie. It was, he decided, the best sick day he could have ever imagined.

### Part 10: The Leviathan's Heart

With their intermission lunch complete and Jordan comfortably resettled on the sofa between Aubrie and Madison, Mark hit "play" once more. The screen lit up with the next title card: Book II: The Shadow's Heart.

The afternoon was consumed by the epic, sprawling narrative. For Hannah, Sarah, and Madison, who only knew the 100-minute theatrical version, this was like watching a completely different film.

"Wait, Kaela has a whole *subplot*?" Sarah whispered, astonished, as they watched Kaela begin her subtle psychological war against Lord Malakor, planting seeds of rebellion within the Shadowspire.

"This makes her so much better than just the damsel in the tower!" Hannah agreed, completely engrossed.

They watched in awe as the *Leviathan Cut* delved into Malakor's internal conflict, his chilling "Sacrifice or Queen?" monologue, and the full, unabridged rock opera performance of "The Tyrant's Throne (Shadow's Heart)". When the famous interruption scene played out—where Malakor implodes his own minion for praising "chaos"—Mark let out a low whistle of appreciation.

Jordan, despite his lingering fever, was completely captivated. The cheesy 80s fantasy bliss was the perfect antidote to his real-world anxieties. He found himself mesmerized not just by the story, but by the music. The soaring synth score by Klaus Richter, the motivational rock of "One Step at a Time (The Gauntlet's Path)," and the dark, theatrical power of Jareth Blade's villain song... it was all so different from anything his mother had ever let him listen to. This 1980s music, with its high drama and electric guitar solos, was starting to spark a genuine interest.

The film reached its emotional low point as Kaelan, having crossed the Bridge of a Thousand Truths, collapsed in the mud, finally understanding his love for his sister. The classic power ballad "I Can't Live Without You (Kaela's Theme)" swelled, and the entire room was silent.

"They cut this down to, like, thirty seconds in the theatrical release," Madison murmured, her eyes misty. "This... this is just beautiful."

As *Book II* drew to a close, Mark paused the film. The group sat in a comfortable, stunned silence. Hours had passed in what felt like minutes.

"Okay," Mark said, his voice a little hoarse. "That's the end of Book II. I think that's a perfect stopping point before dinner."

Right on cue, as if sensing the epic narrative beat, Laura's voice called down from the kitchen. "Dinner, everyone! I hope you're hungry!"

### Part 11: The Intermission Feast

The epic, synthesized score of The Silver Gauntlet of Aethel was replaced by the welcome, real-world sound of Laura Brooks calling down the stairs. "Dinner, everyone!"

"You're not going to want to miss this!" Daniel's voice added. "Your mom made her famous baked ziti!"

A cheerful, hungry groan went through the basement. The group, still in their pajamas, paused the movie and began to stir. As Jordan started to push himself up from the sofa, he felt a little less dizzy than he had at lunchtime.

"Whoa, hold on, sicky," Hannah said, stopping him with a gentle hand on his shoulder. "Temperature check first."

She retrieved the thermometer, and they waited. When it beeped, she checked the reading and smiled, a look of genuine relief on her face. "99.8," she announced. "It's going down. Still a fever, but it's an improvement!".

"See? The 80s cheese is curing him," Mark joked.

The group, insisting on not breaking their movie cocoon, opted to eat in the basement. Laura and Daniel brought down the feast: a massive, steaming casserole dish of baked ziti, a basket of garlic bread, and a large salad. For Jordan, whose appetite was slowly returning with the easing of the fever, Laura brought a small bowl of the ziti alongside his now-standard glass of dry ginger ale.

They ate, their conversation buzzing with the revelations from the *Leviathan Cut*.

"I just can't get over Kaela's entire rebellion subplot," Sarah said, her mouth full of pasta. "In the theatrical release, she's just... there. Here, she's actively waging a psychological war against Malakor!"

"And the 'Sacrifice or Queen?' monologue!" Madison added. "It makes him so much more complex than just a cackling villain. He actually *wants* her to join him!"

Jordan ate his small portion, his anxiety from the I-64 trigger and the IRS letter finally feeling distant. He was still sick, but he was surrounded by his family, his fever was dropping, and he was immersed in a world of epic synthesizer scores and crystal spiders. He was, despite everything, perfectly content.

### Part 12: The Pact of Gratitude

After the hearty baked ziti dinner, a comfortable, sleepy quiet settled over the Brooks' basement. The Group of 8, all still in their pajamas, relocated from the dining area to the main living space. The *Leviathan Cut* of *The Silver Gauntlet of Aethel* was paused, *Book III* waiting patiently for Monday. Mark and Hannah claimed one of the queen-sized portable beds, cuddling up to read companionably.

Jordan, his fever still a low, steady 99.8°F, was settled on the large sectional sofa, a thick blanket draped over his lap. He felt weak from the sickness, but his mind was clear and calm. The day's immersion in 80s fantasy, combined with the profound safety of the basement, had successfully pushed all his anxieties about I-64 and the IRS to the background. He was simply present, surrounded by his family.

He looked at the five women of his pact—Aubrie, Madison, Lauren, Sarah, and Zoe—who were chatting quietly on the other beds. He felt a profound, overwhelming wave of gratitude and a simple, powerful need for comfort.

"Guys?" he asked, his voice quiet but clear in the relaxed room. "I... I'm still feeling a little shaky from the fever. Would you... can we just cuddle for a bit? All of us?"

The request was met with immediate, warm smiles. There was no hesitation.

"Of course, J," Madison said, moving to sit beside him on the sofa, instantly taking his hand.

One by one, the other four women joined them, forsaking the beds to pile onto the sofa with him. It was a familiar, loving "puppy pile". Aubrie, his safest person, settled on his other side, her head resting on his shoulder. Lauren and Sarah curled up at their feet, leaning against his legs, their warmth radiating through the blankets.

Finally, Zoe, her own part in the movie-talk done, came and sat on the cushion next to him. Jordan turned his head to look at her, his eyes full of an emotion that was specific and deep.

"Zoe," he whispered, his voice thick with feeling.

Zoe looked at him, her analytical gaze softening. "Are you feeling worse? Do you need the thermometer?"

"No," Jordan said, shaking his head. "I just... thank you".

She looked puzzled. "For what? I didn't do anything."

"For this," Jordan said, gesturing vaguely to the safe, warm basement around them. "This is your *home*. Your childhood home. And you and your parents...". He had to pause, his throat tightening. "You just... opened it. You took me in when I had nowhere else to go. You and Daniel and Laura, you treated me like I was your own son".

He looked at her, and the gratitude in his eyes was absolute. "I'm just so grateful to you, Zoe. You gave me this. You gave me a place to be safe."

Zoe, who lived in a world of data and logic, was visibly moved. She didn't offer a statistic or a logical reason. She simply leaned her head against his shoulder, a silent, powerful acknowledgment of his words. The six of them sat like that for a long time, a quiet, tangled mass of limbs and blankets, cocooned in the absolute safety of their found family.

As the evening grew later and the inevitable need for sleep began to take over, the comfortable pile began to break apart.

"Alright," Madison sighed, stretching. "Time to actually get into beds."

Aubrie and Madison, as his safest people, instinctively moved toward Jordan's designated bed. But Jordan stopped them with a soft word. "Wait."

He looked at Aubrie, then at Madison, his two anchors, and they both saw the gratitude in his eyes and gave him small, understanding nods. He then turned his gaze back to Zoe.

"Zoe... tonight... would you sleep with me?"

She looked surprised, glancing quickly at Aubrie and Madison, who were both smiling encouragingly. There was no jealousy, only understanding.

"It's just..." Jordan hurried to explain, his gratitude for her family still filling his voice. "Being here. In your house. After everything your parents did for me... I just want to be close to you tonight. It makes me feel safe".

Zoe’s usual pragmatic expression melted completely. She understood. This wasn't about the pact's romantic rotation; it was an act of profound, familial gratitude. "Okay, Jordan," she said softly. "Yes. I will."

The rest of the group found their places. Mark and Hannah took their bed. Aubrie, Madison, Lauren, and Sarah paired off on the remaining two queen mattresses. Zoe helped Jordan transfer from his chair into his bed, and she slid in beside him. He immediately turned on his side, cuddling close, his feverish body relaxing against her. He was home, in the house of the family that had saved him, held by the friend who had made it all possible.

### Part 13: The Social Script Crash

Jordan woke up slowly, disoriented in the dim morning light of the basement. His first sensation was the dull, stubborn heat of his low-grade fever, a persistent, uncomfortable static. His second was a profound, grounding warmth at his back. He wasn't wedged between Aubrie and Madison as he had been when he first fell asleep. He was spooning with someone else.

He turned his head on the pillow, his heart giving a small jolt. It was Zoe. She was already awake, her analytic gray eyes focused on the screen of her mPhone as she read.

He tensed. Oh, no. What did I do? He was in bed with Zoe. His eyes shot across the dark room in a panic, searching for his two safest people.

On the adjacent queen-sized portable bed, Aubrie and Madison were also awake, sitting cross-legged and chatting quietly with Lauren and Sarah. They looked over, sensing he was awake. And they smiled. Not tight, angry smiles. They were the warm, genuine, "good morning" smiles of a normal Sunday.

The operating system in Jordan's mind, already made fragile by the fever and the trauma of the I-64 gauntlet, slammed into a fatal, catastrophic error.

His entire life, his mother had programmed him with one absolute, immutable social script: intimacy is exclusive, possessive, and a weapon. His brain, searching for the correct response file, executed: On_Cuddle_With_Zoe(). It then called the required dependency: Find_Response(Aubrie.Jealousy.dll).

The system returned: null.

It tried again: Find_Response(Madison.Anger.dat).

The system returned: null.

His programming had no file for this. He was cuddling another woman, and the two most important women in his life *did not consider it cheating*. Aubrie's and Madison's simple, happy smiles were a data packet his mind had no way to process. It was a System.IO.FileNotFoundException() at the very core of his social being.

It was too much. It was an act of love and trust so unconditional, so radical, that his entire understanding of the world fractured. A low, wounded sound tore from his throat as the system crashed. A single sob, then another. He began to cry, his body shaking with the overwhelming, terrifying, *positive* input. He was descending into a full, system-failure meltdown.

Zoe felt him begin to shake. Her analytical mind, which ran with the cool, clean logic of a C# program, immediately began processing the new data streams.

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: Processing new input... Thread 1 (Self): IsCuddling(Jordan) == true. Status: Stable. Thread 2 (Observers): Aubrie.IsJealous == false. Madison.IsJealous == false. Thread 2 (Observers): EmotionalState == Supportive. 10-12-2015 07:05:15

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: Data analysis complete. This is an efficient, multi-node support system. I like this data. 10-12-2015 07:05:15

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: WARNING: Thread 3 (Jordan) state change detected. Emitting: Crying. Status: Meltdown_Imminent. 10-12-2015 07:05:16

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: Running diagnostics... Cross-referencing Thread 3 with Thread 2. Hypothesis: Jordan.SocialScript.dll is searching for legacy "Jealousy/Conflict" file. 10-12-2015 07:05:17

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: ZObject.Search(Jordan.SocialScript.dll, "On_Cuddle_With_Zoe_Expect_Confrontation.dat") -\> Returns: null. 10-12-2015 07:05:17

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: A System.IO.FileNotFoundException() is causing a kernel panic in the Jordan.OS. 10-12-2015 07:05:17

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: Conclusion: The data is not the problem. The old, legacy script is the problem. The new system (Pact = true, Jealousy = false) is superior. 10-12-2015 07:05:18

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: Action: Do not alter stable input. Reinforce new, correct data. Execute: ContinueCuddle(Jordan). 10-12-2015 07:05:18

As Jordan’s sobs grew, Zoe didn't pull away. She processed that he was overwhelmed by a logical, positive input he wasn't prepared for. She liked this data; it was an efficient, supportive, multi-vector system. This was a logical extension of the pact, and if it was logical, it was right. This system would be highly efficient for future, long-term family planning.

She calmly tightened her embrace, pulling his trembling body closer. "Shh," she whispered, her voice a low, steady, analytical hum. "The data is fine, Jordan. The system is stable. Your script is just outdated. We are the new variable. Just process the input. You're safe."

Her steady, grounding presence, combined with the complete lack of panic from Aubrie and Madison, was the only thing that could firewall the crash. He was having a meltdown simply because he was, for the first time in his life, unconditionally and completely loved by everyone, without conflict.

### Part 14: The System Crash

Jordan’s meltdown escalated, his sobs turning into raw, panicked gasps as he was held securely by Zoe. He was drowning in a logical impossibility.

From their bed, Aubrie and Madison saw the crash in real time. They saw his terror and knew exactly what it meant. He wasn't afraid *of* Zoe; he was terrified *for* himself, expecting the inevitable punishment for breaking a rule he didn't know how to follow. They knew, instinctively, that observation wasn't enough. They had to provide overwhelming, contradictory data.

Silently, Madison got up from her bed. She climbed onto the queen-sized portable mattress and pressed herself firmly against Jordan's back, wrapping her arms around both him and Zoe, adding her warmth to the cuddle. "We've got you, J," she whispered. "You're okay."

A moment later, Aubrie joined, settling in beside Zoe, effectively cocooning Jordan between the three of them.

This was the final proof. His two absolute safest people were not angry, were not jealous; they were actively *joining in*. The System.IO.FileNotFoundException() exploded, taking his entire operating system down. The positive input was so overwhelming that his brain, lacking any script for this scenario, interpreted it as a critical threat. His meltdown worsened, his whole body shaking violently.

Zoe, still holding him, calmly processed the new data streams.

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: New input detected. Thread 2 (Observers): Aubrie.Action == Execute: Cuddle(Jordan, Zoe). Thread 2 (Observers): Madison.Action == Execute: Cuddle(Jordan, Zoe). 10-12-2015 07:05:20

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: Updating analysis. System is not just 'not-jealous'; system is 'actively-supportive' and 'participatory'. 10-12-2015 07:05:21

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: This data strongly reinforces the (Pact = true, Jealousy = false) variable. This is a highly efficient, load-balanced emotional support system. 10-12-2015 07:05:21

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: WARNING: Thread 3 (Jordan) state change detected. Meltdown intensity increased exponentially. 10-12-2015 07:05:22

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: Running diagnostics... Hypothesis: New input from Aubrie and Madison has compounded the initial System.IO.FileNotFoundException(). Jordan.OS is attempting to process "Cuddle(Zoe) + Cuddle(Aubrie) + Cuddle(Madison)" simultaneously. Error: "SocialScript.dll" cannot handle concurrent, non-conflicting intimacy variables. The system is overwhelmed by positive data. 10-12-2015 07:05:23

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: Action: The system crash is severe. Data reinforcement must be paired with active de-escalation. Execute: MaintainCuddle(Jordan). Execute: Signal(Aubrie, Madison) -\> Initiate: VerbalDe-escalationProtocol. Execute: Delay(Breakfast). 10-12-2015 07:05:24

Upstairs, Daniel and Laura had breakfast ready, but they heard the muffled, raw sounds of Jordan's distress and knew to wait.

Downstairs, the three women—Aubrie, Madison, and Zoe—formed a living fortress around him. They held him tightly, grounding him against the storm his own mind had created. "Shh, you're safe," Aubrie whispered, her voice a low anchor. "This is real. We're not mad. We love you." Madison added, "We're all here. This is good, Jordan. This is family." They didn't try to stop the meltdown; they simply held him securely, offering steady, loving input until his terrified, overloaded system could finally, slowly, reboot.

### Part 15: A New, Stable System

Jordan was completely overwhelmed. The "system crash" was absolute, a profound emotional overload that left him shaking and sobbing in the center of a three-person cuddle. He was trapped in a feedback loop: the very proof that he was safe—the fact that his two "safest people" were *endorsing* his intimacy with Zoe by joining in—was the exact data his legacy social scripts could not compute.

Zoe, still holding him, registered the new data points as Aubrie and Madison pressed in, adding their warmth and reassurance.

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: Executing coordinated de-escalation protocol. Thread 1 (Self): Maintain(Cuddle_Pressure.Firm). Thread 2 (Aubrie): Executing(Verbal_Reassurance.v2.Script, "You're safe, we're not mad"). Thread 2 (Madison): Executing(Verbal_Reassurance.v3.Script, "We love this, this is good"). 10-12-2015 07:05:25

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: Monitoring Thread 3 (Jordan)... Jordan.OS.State == KernelPanic. Running... Jordan.OS.State == Crying. Diminishing... Jordan.OS.State == Shaking. Subsided. Jordan.OS.State == Stable. Post-Crash. SituationalMutism == True. 10-12-2015 07:05:27

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: De-escalation successful. Meltdown has passed. 10-12-2015 07:05:28

ZOE.Systems.Logs.KernelLog: The "Pact = true, Jealousy = false" variable is now considered a stable, foundational axiom of the system. 10-12-2015 07:05:29

It was a long, slow process. The three women held him, speaking in low, gentle, murmuring tones, offering a constant, steady stream of positive, physical, and verbal data. Gradually, the violent, racking sobs subsided into quiet, exhausted tremors. The storm had passed, leaving him utterly drained and non-verbal, his ability to speak temporarily locked away by the sheer intensity of the experience.

The rest of the group had given them space, but now, hearing the quiet, Daniel called down the stairs. "Everyone okay down there? Breakfast is ready."

The group was, in fact, starving. As the trio on the bed slowly untangled, Hannah moved in with the thermometer for a temperature check. "99.1," she announced with a relieved smile. "It's definitely going down".

Daniel and Laura brought down the trays of food. For the group, it was scrambled eggs, bacon, and toast. For Jordan, seeing his fever was breaking and his appetite returning, Laura had prepared something more substantial than just soup: a small bowl of oatmeal with brown sugar and a piece of dry toast, alongside his requisite ginger ale.

Jordan sat up, cocooned in blankets between Aubrie and Madison, and ate every bite. He felt weak, but the profound sense of peace and security was a more powerful tonic than any medicine. He was exhausted, but he was also fascinated by the movie. He pulled out his mPhone and typed a note, showing it to Aubrie:

"Can we finish the movie? I need to see how Kaelan and Kaela's story ends."

"Absolutely," she said, her smile brilliant.

The group began to resettle. Mark, who had been quietly observing the morning's intense emotional display, came over as Jordan finished his breakfast. He understood, on a level Tom never could have, that this pact wasn't a threat; it was a testament to the trust they had all built.

"Just so you know, J," Mark said, his voice full of the easy, platonic love he had for his friend. "What you have with the girls is amazing. And that trust... it goes for *all* of us. Hannah and I both love you to pieces. You are always, always welcome to hug and cuddle with her, just like you do with me. We cherish that. You're our brother".

Hannah, standing beside him, beamed and nodded her complete agreement. "Always, Jordan. You're our family".
